</div></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_language_regulators" title="List of language regulators">Regulated by</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Russian_Language_Institute" title="Russian Language Institute">Russian Language Institute</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[23]</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="color: black; background-color:  #c9ffd9;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-1" title="ISO 639-1">ISO 639-1</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/php/langcodes_name.php?iso_639_1=ru" rel="nofollow">ru</a></span></code></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-2" title="ISO 639-2">ISO 639-2</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/php/langcodes_name.php?code_ID=379" rel="nofollow">rus</a></span></code></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a class="extiw" href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/rus" title="iso639-3:rus">rus</a></code></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></i></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/russ1263" rel="nofollow">russ1263</a></code></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code>53-AAA-ea &lt; <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">53-AAA-e</a><br/>(varieties: 53-AAA-eaa to 53-AAA-eat)</code></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png"><img alt="GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png" data-file-height="2579" data-file-width="5004" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png/350px-GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png/525px-GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png/700px-GeographicalExtentOfRussianLanguage.png 2x" width="350"/></a><div style="text-align:left;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0080ff; color:black;"> </span> Russian is a majority language</span><br/>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#88c4ff; color:black;"> </span> Russian is a minority language</span><br/>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#4488ca; color:black;"> </span> Russian is a spoken language</span><br/></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Russian</b> (Russian: <span lang="ru">русский язык</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">russkij jazyk</i></span>) is an <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">East Slavic language</a> mainly spoken across Russia. It is the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a>, and belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language family</a>. It is one of four living East Slavic languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[j]</a></sup> and is also a part of the larger <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic languages</a>. Besides Russia itself, Russian is an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> in Belarus, Kazakhstan, and Kyrgyzstan, and is used widely as a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> throughout the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Central Asia, and to some extent in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope-36">[26]</a></sup> It was the <a href="/wiki/De_facto#National_languages" title="De facto"><i>de facto</i> language</a> of the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USSR_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-USSR-37">[27]</a></sup> and continues to be used in public life with varying proficiency in all of the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">post-Soviet states</a>.
</p><p>Russian has over 258 million total speakers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[28]</a></sup> It is the most spoken <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-language_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-language-39">[29]</a></sup> and the most spoken <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">native language</a> in Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[30]</a></sup> as well as the most geographically widespread language of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-language_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-language-39">[29]</a></sup> It is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">seventh-most spoken language by number of native speakers</a>, and the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_total_number_of_speakers" title="List of languages by total number of speakers">eighth-most spoken language by total number of speakers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[31]</a></sup> Russian is one of two official languages aboard the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[32]</a></sup> as well as one of the six <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official languages of the United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Russian is written using the <a href="/wiki/Russian_alphabet" title="Russian alphabet">Russian alphabet</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a>; it distinguishes between <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatal</a> <a href="/wiki/Secondary_articulation" title="Secondary articulation">secondary articulation</a> and those without—the so-called "soft" and "hard" sounds. Almost every consonant has a hard or soft counterpart, and the distinction is a prominent feature of the language. Another important aspect is the <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">reduction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">unstressed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">Stress</a>, which is often unpredictable, is not normally indicated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthographically" title="Orthographically">orthographically</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETimberlake200417_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETimberlake200417-44">[34]</a></sup> though an optional <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> may be used to mark stress – such as to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Homograph" title="Homograph">homographic</a> words (e.g. <a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%97%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%BA_(%D1%83%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B9%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%BE)" title="ru:Замок (устройство)">замо́к</a> [<i>zamók</i> – a 'lock'] and <a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%97%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%BA_(%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5)" title="ru:Замок (строение)">за́мок</a> [<i>zámok</i> – a 'castle']), or to indicate the proper pronunciation of uncommon words or names.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Russian is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Slavic_language" title="East Slavic language">East Slavic language</a> of the wider <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European family</a>. It is a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Old_East_Slavic" title="Old East Slavic">Old East Slavic</a>, a language used in <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, which was a loose conglomerate of <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">East Slavic</a> tribes from the late 9th to the mid 13th centuries. From the point of view of <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken language</a>, its closest relatives are <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rusyn_language" title="Rusyn language">Rusyn</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[35]</a></sup> the other three languages in the East Slavic branch. In many places in eastern and southern Ukraine and throughout Belarus, these languages are spoken interchangeably, and in certain areas traditional bilingualism resulted in language mixtures such as <a href="/wiki/Surzhyk" title="Surzhyk">Surzhyk</a> in eastern Ukraine and <a href="/wiki/Trasianka" title="Trasianka">Trasianka</a> in Belarus. An East Slavic <a href="/wiki/Old_Novgorod_dialect" title="Old Novgorod dialect">Old Novgorod dialect</a>, although it vanished during the 15th or 16th century, is sometimes considered to have played a significant role in the formation of modern Russian. Also, Russian has notable lexical similarities with <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> due to a common <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a> influence on both languages, but because of later interaction in the 19th and 20th centuries, Bulgarian grammar differs markedly from Russian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006477–478,_480_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006477–478,_480-46">[36]</a></sup> In the 19th century (in Russia until 1917), the language was often called "<a href="/wiki/Great_Russian_language" title="Great Russian language">Great Russian</a>" to distinguish it from Belarusian, then called "White Russian" and Ukrainian, then called "Little Russian".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a> (mainly abstract and literary words), principles of word formations, and, to some extent, inflections and literary style of Russian have been also influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic</a>, a developed and partly Russified form of the <a href="/wiki/South_Slavic_languages" title="South Slavic languages">South Slavic</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> language used by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>. However, the East Slavic forms have tended to be used exclusively in the various dialects that are experiencing a rapid decline. In some cases, both the East Slavic and the <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a> forms are in use, with many different meanings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <i>For details, see <a href="/wiki/Russian_phonology" title="Russian phonology">Russian phonology</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russian_language" title="History of the Russian language">History of the Russian language</a>.</i>
</p><p>Over the course of centuries, the vocabulary and literary style of Russian have also been influenced by Western and Central European languages such as Greek, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, German, French, Italian, and English,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[37]</a></sup> and to a lesser extent the languages to the south and the east: <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[41]</a></sup> Arabic, and <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Defense_Language_Institute" title="Defense Language Institute">Defense Language Institute</a> in <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>, Russian is classified as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DLI_levels" title="DLI levels">level III language</a> in terms of learning difficulty for native English speakers, requiring approximately 1,100 hours of immersion instruction to achieve intermediate fluency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[43]</a></sup> It is also regarded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Intelligence_Community" title="United States Intelligence Community">United States Intelligence Community</a> as a "hard target" language, due to both its difficulty to master for English speakers and its critical role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_foreign_policy" title="American foreign policy">U.S. world policy</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Russian">Standard Russian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Standard Russian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moscow_dialect" title="Moscow dialect">Moscow dialect</a></div>
<p>Feudal divisions and conflicts between rival polities created obstacles to the exchange of goods and ideas between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_medieval" title="Early medieval">early medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a> principalities before and especially during Mongol rule. This strengthened dialectal differences and for centuries prevented the establishment of any standardized "national" language. The gradual but steady emergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Principality_of_Moscow" title="Grand Principality of Moscow">Grand Principality of Moscow</a> (1263–1547) – later the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a> from 1547 – as the dominant and ever-expanding polity of the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus' people">Rus'</a>, necessitated the earliest attempts at standardization of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Slavic_language" title="East Slavic language">East Slavic language</a> based on the Moscow dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[44]</a></sup> Since then the trend of language policy in Russia has been standardization in both the restricted sense of reducing dialectical barriers between ethnic Russians, and the broader sense of expanding the use of Russian alongside or in favour of other languages that exist within the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, and the later <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and Russian Federation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current standard form of Russian is generally regarded as the <i>modern Russian literary language</i> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">современный русский литературный язык</span></span> – "sovremenny russky literaturny yazyk"). It arose in the beginning of the 18th century with the modernization reforms of the Russian state under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> and developed from the Moscow (<a href="/wiki/Central_Russian_dialects" title="Central Russian dialects">Middle or Central Russian</a>) dialect substratum under the influence of some of the previous century's Russian chancery language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[44]</a></sup> This occurred in spite of the fact that <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, the Western-oriented capital created by the "<a href="/wiki/Westernization" title="Westernization">Westernizing</a>" Tsar <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a>, being the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> for over 200 years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a> compiled the first book of <a href="/wiki/Russian_grammar" title="Russian grammar">Russian grammar</a> aimed at standardization in 1755. The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy" title="Russian Academy">Russian Academy</a>'s first explanatory Russian dictionary appeared in 1783. In the 18th and the late 19th centuries, a period known as the "Golden Age" of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Literature" title="Russian Literature">Russian Literature</a>, the grammar, vocabulary, and pronunciation of the Russian language in a standardized literary form emerged.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Prior to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik_Revolution" title="Bolshevik Revolution">Bolshevik Revolution</a>, the spoken form of the Russian language was that of the nobility and the urban bourgeoise. Russian peasants, the great majority of the population, continued to speak in their own dialects. However, the peasants' speech was never systematically studied, as it was generally regarded by philologists as simply a source of folklore and an object of curiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[45]</a></sup> This was acknowledged by the noted Russian dialectologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nikolai_Karinsky&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nikolai Karinsky (page does not exist)">Nikolai Karinsky</a> (1873–1935), who toward the end of his life wrote: “Scholars of Russian dialects mostly studied phonetics and morphology. Some scholars and collectors compiled local dictionaries.... We have almost no studies of lexical material or the syntax of Russian dialects.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1917, Marxist linguists had no interest in the multiplicity of peasant dialects and regarded their language as a relic of the rapidly disappearing past that was not worthy of scholarly attention. Nakhimovsky quotes the Soviet academicians A.M Ivanov and L.P Yakubinsky, writing in 1930:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The language of peasants has a motley diversity inherited from feudalism.... On its way to becoming proletariat peasantry brings to the factory and the industrial plant their local peasant dialects with their phonetics, grammar and vocabulary... the very process of recruiting workers from peasants and the mobility of worker population generate another process: the liquidation of peasant inheritance by way of leveling the particulars of local dialects. On the ruins of peasant multilingua, in the context of developing heavy industry, a qualitatively new entity can be said to emerge—the general language of the working class... capitalism has the tendency of creating the general urban language of a given society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[47]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>By the mid-20th century, such dialects were forced out with the introduction of the compulsory education system that was established by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_government" title="Soviet government">Soviet government</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite the formalization of Standard Russian, some nonstandard dialectal features (such as fricative <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɣ]</span> in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Russian_dialects" title="Southern Russian dialects">Southern Russian dialects</a>) are still observed in colloquial speech.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_Russian_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of Russian speakers">Geographical distribution of Russian speakers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="541" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg/220px-Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg/330px-Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg/440px-Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ru%C5%A1tina_ve_sv%C4%9Bt%C4%9B.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hemisphere view of countries where Russian is an official language and countries where it is spoken as a first or second language by at least 30% of the population but is not an official language</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="710" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG/400px-Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG/600px-Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russian_ex-USSR_2004.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Competence of Russian in countries of the former Soviet Union (except Russia), 2004</div></div></div>
<p>In 2010, there were 259.8 million speakers of Russian in the world: in Russia – 137.5 million, in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">CIS</a> and Baltic countries – 93.7 million, in Eastern Europe – 12.9 million, Western Europe – 7.3 million, Asia – 2.7 million, Middle East and North Africa – 1.3 million, Sub-Saharan Africa – 0.1 million, Latin America – 0.2 million, U.S., Canada, Australia and New Zealand – 4.1 million speakers. Therefore, the Russian language is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="Languages by number of native speakers">seventh-largest in the world by number of speakers</a>, after English, Mandarin, Hindi-Urdu, Spanish, French, Arabic and Portuguese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope.ru_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope.ru-58">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-rating-2018_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-rating-2018-59">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Арефьев_А._Л._Сжимающееся_русскоязычие_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Арефьев_А._Л._Сжимающееся_русскоязычие-60">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Russian is one of the <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">six official languages</a> of the United Nations. Education in Russian is still a popular choice for both Russian as a second language (RSL) and native speakers in Russia, and in many former Soviet republics. Russian is still seen as an important language for children to learn in most of the former Soviet republics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallup2008_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-gallup2008-61">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="621" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg/240px-Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg/360px-Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg/480px-Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ukraine_census_2001_Russian.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of people in Ukraine with Russian as their native language (according to a 2001 census) (<a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Ukraine" title="Administrative divisions of Ukraine">by region</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>In Belarus, Russian is a second state language alongside Belarusian per the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Belarus" title="Constitution of Belarus">Constitution of Belarus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 77% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 67% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Estonia, Russian is spoken by 29.6% of the population according to a 2011 estimate from the World Factbook,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookoffact_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-bookoffact-64">[54]</a></sup> and is officially considered a foreign language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> School education in the Russian language is a very contentious point in Estonian politics, but as of 2019 promises have been given that such schools will remain open in the near future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Latvia, Russian is officially considered a foreign language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 55% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 26% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup> On February 18, 2012, Latvia held a <a href="/wiki/2012_Latvian_constitutional_referendum" title="2012 Latvian constitutional referendum">constitutional referendum</a> on whether to adopt Russian as a second official language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[56]</a></sup> According to the Central Election Commission, 74.8% voted against, 24.9% voted for and the voter turnout was 71.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[57]</a></sup> Starting in 2019, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language_in_Latvia" title="Russian language in Latvia">instruction in Russian</a> will be gradually discontinued in private colleges and universities in Latvia, and in general instruction in Latvian public high schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (January 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Lithuania, Russian has no official or any legal status, but the use of the language has some presence in certain areas. A large part of the population, especially the older generations, can speak Russian as a foreign language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[60]</a></sup> However, English has replaced Russian as <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> in Lithuania and around 80% of young people speak English as the first foreign language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[61]</a></sup> In contrast to the other two Baltic states, Lithuania has a relatively small Russian-speaking minority (5.0% as of 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andrlik_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-andrlik-72">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Moldova, Russian is considered to be the language of inter-ethnic communication under a Soviet-era law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 50% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 19% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2010)" title="Russian Census (2010)">2010 census in Russia</a>, Russian language skills were indicated by 138 million people (99.4% of the respondents), while according to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2002)" title="Russian Census (2002)">2002 census</a> – 142.6 million people (99.2% of the respondents).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Ukraine, Russian is a significant minority language. According to estimates from Demoskop Weekly, in 2004 there were 14,400,000 native speakers of Russian in the country, and 29 million active speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope251_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope251-74">[64]</a></sup> 65% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 38% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup> On September 5, 2017, Ukraine's Parliament passed a <a href='/wiki/Ukrainization#2017_law_"On_Education"' title="Ukrainization">new education law</a> which bars primary education to all students in any language but <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[65]</a></sup> The law faced criticism from officials in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, Russian was a mandatory language taught in the schools of the members of the old <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> and in other <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">countries</a> that used to be satellites of the USSR. According to the Eurobarometer 2005 survey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[68]</a></sup> fluency in Russian remains fairly high (20–40%) in some countries, in particular those where the people speak a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_language" title="Slavic language">Slavic language</a> and thereby have an edge in learning Russian<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="give some clear explanations and citations where other Slavic speakers claim how Russian is far easier for them than for example english (August 2020)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (namely, Poland, Czech Republic, Slovakia, and Bulgaria).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Significant Russian-speaking groups also exist in Western Europe. These have been fed by several waves of immigrants since the beginning of the 20th century, each with its own flavor of language. The United Kingdom, Germany, Finland, Spain, Portugal, France, Italy, Belgium, Greece, Norway, and Austria have significant Russian-speaking communities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Armenia, Russian has no official status, but it is recognized as a minority language under the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 30% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 2% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Azerbaijan, Russian has no official status, but is a <i>lingua franca</i> of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 26% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 5% used it as the main language with family, friends, or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, Russian has no official status, but it is spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Russians_in_China" title="Russians in China">small Russian communities</a> in the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">Heilongjiang</a> and the northwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Uyghur_Autonomous_Region" title="Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region">Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region</a>.
</p><p>In Georgia, Russian has no official status, but it is recognized as a minority language under the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-6"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> Russian is the language of 9% of the population according to the World Factbook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[69]</a></sup> Ethnologue cites Russian as the country's de facto working language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethn_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethn-80">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Kazakhstan, Russian is not a state language, but according to article 7 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kazakhstan" title="Constitution of Kazakhstan">Constitution of Kazakhstan</a> its usage enjoys equal status to that of the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh language</a> in state and local administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-7"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> The 2009 census reported that 10,309,500 people, or 84.8% of the population aged 15 and above, could read and write well in Russian, and understand the spoken language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kazcensus_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-kazcensus-81">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Kyrgyzstan, Russian is a co-official language per article 5 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Kyrgyzstan" title="Constitution of Kyrgyzstan">Constitution of Kyrgyzstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-8"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> The 2009 census states that 482,200 people speak Russian as a native language, or 8.99% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyrcen_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-kyrcen-82">[72]</a></sup> Additionally, 1,854,700 residents of Kyrgyzstan aged 15 and above fluently speak Russian as a second language, or 49.6% of the population in the age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyrcen_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-kyrcen-82">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Tajikistan, Russian is the language of inter-ethnic communication under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Tajikistan" title="Constitution of Tajikistan">Constitution of Tajikistan</a> and is permitted in official documentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-9"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> 28% of the population was fluent in Russian in 2006, and 7% used it as the main language with family, friends or at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope329_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope329-63">[53]</a></sup> The World Factbook notes that Russian is widely used in government and business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookoffact_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-bookoffact-64">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Turkmenistan, Russian lost its status as the official <i>lingua franca</i> in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-10"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup> Among 12%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookoffact_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-bookoffact-64">[54]</a></sup> of the population who grew up in the Soviet era can speak Russian, other generations of citizens that do not have any knowledge of Russian. Primary and secondary education by Russian is almost non-existent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[73]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the Turkmen state press and newspaper <a href="/wiki/Neytralny_Turkmenistan" title="Neytralny Turkmenistan">Neytralny Turkmenistan</a> regularly publish material version in Russian-language, and there are schools like <a href="/wiki/Joint_Turkmen-Russian_Secondary_School" title="Joint Turkmen-Russian Secondary School">Joint Turkmen-Russian Secondary School</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In Uzbekistan, Russian is the language of inter-ethnic communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-AA-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Шухрат_Хуррамов_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Шухрат_Хуррамов-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AB_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-AB-11">[9]</a></sup> It has some official roles, being permitted in official documentation and is the <i>lingua franca</i> of the country and the language of the elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundeh1_62-11"><a href="#cite_note-fundeh1-62">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UZB_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-UZB-84">[74]</a></sup> Russian is spoken by 14.2% of the population according to an undated estimate from the World Factbook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookoffact_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-bookoffact-64">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, Russian was the most widely taught foreign language in Mongolia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[75]</a></sup> and was compulsory in Year 7 onward as a second foreign language in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-30">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Russian is also spoken in Israel. The number of native Russian-speaking Israelis numbers around 1.5 million Israelis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[76]</a></sup> 15% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[77]</a></sup> The Israeli <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">press</a> and websites regularly publish material in Russian and there are Russian newspapers, television stations, schools, and social media outlets based in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[78]</a></sup> There is an Israeli TV channel mainly broadcasting in Russian with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israel_Plus" title="Israel Plus">Israel Plus</a>. See also <a href="/wiki/Russian_language_in_Israel" title="Russian language in Israel">Russian language in Israel</a>.
</p><p>Russian is also spoken as a second language by a small number of people in Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Vietnam, Russian has been added in the elementary curriculum along with Chinese and Japanese and were named as "first foreign languages" for Vietnamese students to learn, on equal footing with English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russian_language_in_the_United_States" title="Russian language in the United States">Russian language in the United States</a></div>
<p>The language was first introduced in North America when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_explorers" title="Russian explorers">Russian explorers</a> voyaged into <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and claimed it for Russia during the 18th century. Although most Russian colonists left after the United States bought the land in 1867, a handful stayed and preserved the Russian language in this region to this day, although only a few elderly speakers of this unique dialect are left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[81]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nikolaevsk,_Alaska" title="Nikolaevsk, Alaska">Nikolaevsk, Alaska</a>, Russian is more spoken than English. Sizable Russian-speaking communities also exist in North America, especially in large urban centers of the US and Canada, such as <a href="/wiki/Russian_Americans_in_New_York_City" title="Russian Americans in New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, Los Angeles, <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>, San Francisco, <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spokane,_Washington" title="Spokane, Washington">Spokane</a>, Toronto, <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russians_in_Baltimore" title="History of the Russians in Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, Miami, Chicago, <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>. In a number of locations they issue their own newspapers, and live in <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_enclave" title="Ethnic enclave">ethnic enclaves</a> (especially the generation of immigrants who started arriving in the early 1960s). Only about 25% of them are ethnic Russians, however. Before the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a>, the overwhelming majority of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russophone" title="Russophone">Russophones</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brighton_Beach,_Brooklyn" title="Brighton Beach, Brooklyn">Brighton Beach, Brooklyn</a> in New York City were Russian-speaking Jews. Afterward, the influx from the countries of the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> changed the statistics somewhat, with ethnic Russians and Ukrainians immigrating along with some more Russian Jews and Central Asians. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">United States Census</a>, in 2007 Russian was the primary language spoken in the homes of over 850,000 individuals living in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the second half of the 20th century, Russian was the most popular foreign language in Cuba. Besides being taught at universities and schools, there were also educational programs on the radio and TV. However, starting January 2019 Cuban television opened an educational program devoted to the Russian language. This project is fully entitled to be called an anticipated one, because the Russian – Cuban collaboration is a strategic direction actively developed as more and more young people are interested in the Russian language, the Education navigator informs. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Havana_State_University&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Havana State University (page does not exist)">Havana State University</a> has started a bachelor's specialization called the Russian Language and the Second Foreign Language. There is also the Russian language department, where students can scrutinize e-books without internet connection. Additional courses on the Russian language are open at two schools of the Cuban capital city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[83]</a></sup> An estimated 200,000 people speak the Russian language in Cuba, on the account that more than 23,000 Cubans who took higher studies in the former Soviet Union and later in Russia, and another important group of people who studied at military schools and technologists, plus the nearly 2,000 Russians residing in Cuba and their descendants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_international_language">As an international language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: As an international language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russophone" title="Russophone">Russophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_official_languages_by_institution" title="List of official languages by institution">List of official languages by institution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Internet_in_Russian" title="Internet in Russian">Internet in Russian</a></div>
<p>Russian is one of the official languages (or has similar status and interpretation must be provided into Russian) of the following:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col">
<ul><li>United Nations</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Intellectual_Property_Organization" title="World Intellectual Property Organization">World Intellectual Property Organization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">International Telecommunication Union</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">International Fund for Agricultural Development</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Universal_Postal_Union" title="Universal Postal Union">Universal Postal Union</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">Commonwealth of Independent States</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Cooperation_Organisation" title="Shanghai Cooperation Organisation">Shanghai Cooperation Organisation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Economic_Community" title="Eurasian Economic Community">Eurasian Economic Community</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Collective_Security_Treaty_Organization" title="Collective Security Treaty Organization">Collective Security Treaty Organization</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_Secretariat" title="Antarctic Treaty Secretariat">Antarctic Treaty Secretariat</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Mathematical_Olympiad" title="International Mathematical Olympiad">International Mathematical Olympiad</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> (defunct)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Comecon" title="Comecon">Council for Mutual Economic Assistance</a> (defunct)</li></ul>
</div>
<p>The Russian language is also one of two official languages aboard the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> – <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> astronauts who serve alongside Russian cosmonauts usually take Russian language courses. This practice goes back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo-Soyuz" title="Apollo-Soyuz">Apollo-Soyuz</a> mission, which first flew in 1975.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In March 2013, it was announced that Russian is now the second-most used language on the Internet after English. People use the Russian language on 5.9% of all websites, slightly ahead of German and far behind English (54.7%). Russian is used not only on 89.8% of <a href="/wiki/.ru" title=".ru">.ru</a> sites, but also on 88.7% of sites with the former Soviet Union domain <a href="/wiki/.su" title=".su">.su</a>. The websites of former Soviet Union nations also use high levels of Russian: 79.0% in Ukraine, 86.9% in Belarus, 84.0% in Kazakhstan, 79.6% in Uzbekistan, 75.9% in Kyrgyzstan and 81.8% in Tajikistan. However, Russian is the sixth-most used language on the top 1,000 sites, behind English, Chinese, French, German, and Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Russian_dialects" title="Russian dialects">Russian dialects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moscow_dialect" title="Moscow dialect">Moscow dialect</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pomor_dialect" title="Pomor dialect">Pomor dialect</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1723" data-file-width="1465" decoding="async" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png/300px-Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png/450px-Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png/600px-Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dialects_of_Russian_language-ru.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian dialects in 1915 <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; color:black;"> </span> 8. <a href="/wiki/Oryol" title="Oryol">Orel</a> (Don) dialect</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF7D26;
