color:black;"> </span> 12. Northern Russian dialect with <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> influences</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ECBD00; color:black;"> </span> 13. Sloboda and Steppe dialects of <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD93E; color:black;"> </span> 14. Steppe dialect of Ukrainian with Russian influences</div> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div></div>
<p>Russian is a rather homogeneous language, in dialectal variation, due to the early political centralization under Moscow's rule, compulsory education, mass migration from rural to urban areas in the 20th century, and other factors. The standard language is used in written and spoken form almost everywhere in the country, from <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad" title="Kaliningrad">Kaliningrad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> in the West to <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petropavlovsk-Kamchatsky" title="Petropavlovsk-Kamchatsky">Petropavlovsk-Kamchatsky</a> in the East, the enormous distance between notwithstanding.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Dialect_levelling" title="Dialect levelling">leveling</a> after 1900, especially in matters of vocabulary and phonetics, a number of dialects still exist in Russia. Some linguists divide the dialects of Russian into two primary regional groupings, "Northern" and "Southern", with Moscow lying on the zone of transition between the two. Others divide the language into three groupings, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Russian_dialects" title="Northern Russian dialects">Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Russian_dialects" title="Central Russian dialects">Central</a> (or Middle), and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Russian_dialects" title="Southern Russian dialects">Southern</a>, with Moscow lying in the Central region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup> All dialects are also divided into two main chronological categories: the dialects of <i>primary formation</i> (the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a> roughly consists of the modern <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Federal_District" title="Northwestern Federal District">Northwestern</a> Federal districts) and <i>secondary formation</i> (other territories where Russian was brought by migrants from primary formation territories or adopted by the local population). <a href="/wiki/Dialectology" title="Dialectology">Dialectology</a> within Russia recognizes dozens of smaller-scale variants. The dialects often show distinct and non-standard features of pronunciation and intonation, vocabulary, and grammar. Some of these are relics of ancient usage now completely discarded by the standard language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Russian_dialects" title="Northern Russian dialects">Northern Russian dialects</a> and those spoken along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga River</a> typically pronounce unstressed <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> clearly, a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction_in_Russian#Back_vowels" title="Vowel reduction in Russian">okanye</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">оканье</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup> Besides the absence of vowel reduction, some dialects have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_vowel" title="High vowel">high</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongal</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e⁓i̯ɛ/</span> in place of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a> *<i>ě</i> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o⁓u̯ɔ/</span> in stressed closed syllables (as in Ukrainian) instead of Standard Russian <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup> Another Northern dialectal morphological feature is a post-posed definite article <i>-to</i>, <i>-ta</i>, <i>-te</i> similarly to that existing in Bulgarian and Macedonian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Russian_dialects" title="Southern Russian dialects">Southern Russian dialects</a>, instances of unstressed <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> following <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalized</a> consonants and preceding a stressed syllable are not reduced to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ]</span> (as occurs in the Moscow dialect), being instead pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[a]</span> in such positions (e.g. <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%B8#Russian" title="wikt:несли">несл<b>и</b></a></span> is pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[nʲaˈslʲi]</span>, not <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[nʲɪsˈlʲi]</span>) – this is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yakanye" title="Yakanye">yakanye</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">яканье</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[87]</a></sup> Consonants include a <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">fricative <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɣ/</span></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">semivowel <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w⁓u̯/</span></a> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x⁓xv⁓xw/</span>, whereas the Standard and Northern dialects have the consonants <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span>, and final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup> The morphology features a palatalized final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʲ/</span> in 3rd person forms of verbs (this is unpalatalized in the Standard and Northern dialects).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526_96-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussexCubberley2006521–526-96">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[88]</a></sup> Some of these features such as akanye and yakanye, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debuccalized" title="Debuccalized">debuccalized</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenited" title="Lenited">lenited</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">semivowel <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w⁓u̯/</span></a> and palatalized final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʲ/</span> in 3rd person forms of verbs are also present in modern <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> and some dialects of <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> (Eastern <a href="/wiki/Polesia" title="Polesia">Polesian</a>), indicating a linguistic continuum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Old_Novgorod_dialect" title="Old Novgorod dialect">Veliky Novgorod</a> has historically displayed a feature called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chokanye" title="Chokanye">chokanye</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsokanye" title="Tsokanye">tsokanye</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">чоканье</span></span> or <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">цоканье</span></span>), in which <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tɕ/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ts/</span> were switched or merged. So, <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D1%86%D0%B0%D0%BF%D0%BB%D1%8F#Russian" title="wikt:цапля"><b>ц</b>апля</a></span> (tsaplya, 'heron') has been recorded as <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">чапля</span></span> (chaplya). Also, the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_second_palatalization" title="Slavic second palatalization">second palatalization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velars</a> did not occur there, so the so-called <b>ě²</b> (from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a> diphthong *ai) did not cause <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k, ɡ, x/</span> to shift to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ts, dz, s/</span>; therefore, where Standard Russian has <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BF%D1%8C#Russian" title="wikt:цепь"><b>ц</b>епь</a></span> ('chain'), the form <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><b>к</b>епь</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kʲepʲ]</span> is attested in earlier texts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Among the first to study Russian dialects was <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Lomonosov</a> in the 18th century. In the 19th, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Dal" title="Vladimir Dal">Vladimir Dal</a> compiled the first dictionary that included dialectal vocabulary. Detailed mapping of Russian dialects began at the turn of the 20th century. In modern times, the monumental <i>Dialectological Atlas of the Russian Language</i> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Диалектологический атлас русского языка</span></span> – <i>Dialektologichesky atlas russkogo yazyka</i>), was published in three folio volumes 1986–1989, after four decades of preparatory work.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_other_Slavic_languages">Comparison with other Slavic languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Comparison with other Slavic languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic_language" title="Proto-Slavic language">Proto-Slavic</a> (Common Slavic) times all Slavs spoke one mutually intelligible language or group of dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[89]</a></sup> There is a high degree of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> between Russian, <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, and a moderate degree of it across all modern Slavic languages, at least at the conversational level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_languages">Derived languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Derived languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Russian+language%22" rel="nofollow">"Russian language"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Russian+language%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Russian+language%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Russian+language%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Russian+language%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Russian+language%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Balachka" title="Balachka">Balachka</a>, a dialect spoken in Krasnodar region, Don, <a href="/wiki/Kuban" title="Kuban">Kuban</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terek_River" title="Terek River">Terek</a>, brought by relocated <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">Cossacks</a> in 1793 and is based on the southwest Ukrainian dialect. During the Russification of the aforementioned regions in the 1920s to 1950s, it was replaced by the Russian language.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Esperanto_etymology" title="Esperanto etymology">Esperanto</a> has some words of Russian and Slavic origin and some features of its grammar could be derived from Russian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kiselman_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kiselman-102">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fenya" title="Fenya">Fenya</a>, a criminal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argot" title="Argot">argot</a> of ancient origin, with Russian grammar, but with distinct vocabulary</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lojban" title="Lojban">Lojban</a>, Russian is one of its six source languages, weighed for the number of Russian speakers in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lojban_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lojban-103">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medny_Aleut_language" title="Medny Aleut language">Medny Aleut language</a>, an extinct <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed language</a> that was spoken on <a href="/wiki/Bering_Island" title="Bering Island">Bering Island</a> and is characterized by its <a href="/wiki/Aleut" title="Aleut">Aleut</a> nouns and Russian verbs</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Padonkaffsky_jargon" title="Padonkaffsky jargon">Padonkaffsky jargon</a>, a slang language developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Padonki" title="Padonki">padonki</a> of <a href="/wiki/Runet" title="Runet">Runet</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quelia" title="Quelia">Quelia</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Macaronic_language" title="Macaronic language">macaronic language</a> with Russian-derived basic structure and part of the <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a> (mainly nouns and verbs) borrowed from German</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Runglish" title="Runglish">Runglish</a>, a Russian-English pidgin. This word is also used by English speakers to describe the way in which Russians attempt to speak English using Russian morphology and/or syntax.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russenorsk_language" title="Russenorsk language">Russenorsk</a>, an extinct <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> language with mostly Russian vocabulary and mostly <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> grammar, used for communication between <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> and Norwegian traders in the Pomor trade in <a href="/wiki/Finnmark" title="Finnmark">Finnmark</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Surzhyk" title="Surzhyk">Surzhyk</a>, a range of mixed (macaronic) sociolects of Ukrainian and Russian languages used in certain regions of Ukraine and adjacent lands.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trasianka" title="Trasianka">Trasianka</a>, a heavily russified variety of <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> used by a large portion of the rural population in Belarus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taimyr_Pidgin_Russian" title="Taimyr Pidgin Russian">Taimyr Pidgin Russian</a>, spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganasan</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Taymyr_Peninsula" title="Taymyr Peninsula">Taimyr Peninsula</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alphabet">Alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Russian_alphabet" title="Russian alphabet">Russian alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Braille" title="Russian Braille">Russian Braille</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png/220px-Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png/330px-Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Azbuka_1574_by_Ivan_Fyodorov.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from Azbuka (<a href="/wiki/Alphabet_book" title="Alphabet book">Alphabet book</a>), the first East Slavic printed textbook. Printed by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Fyodorov_(printer)" title="Ivan Fyodorov (printer)">Ivan Fyodorov</a> in 1574 in Lviv. This page features the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Russian is written using a <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic</a> alphabet. The Russian alphabet consists of 33 letters. The following table gives their upper case forms, along with <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">IPA</a> values for each letter's typical sound:
</p>
<table cellpadding="4" style="margin:auto; text-align:center;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/A_(Cyrillic)" title="A (Cyrillic)">А</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Be_(Cyrillic)" title="Be (Cyrillic)">Б</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ve_(Cyrillic)" title="Ve (Cyrillic)">В</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ge_(Cyrillic)" title="Ge (Cyrillic)">Г</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/De_(Cyrillic)" title="De (Cyrillic)">Д</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ye_(Cyrillic)" title="Ye (Cyrillic)">Е</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/je/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Yo_(Cyrillic)" title="Yo (Cyrillic)">Ё</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jo/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Zhe_(Cyrillic)" title="Zhe (Cyrillic)">Ж</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʐ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ze_(Cyrillic)" title="Ze (Cyrillic)">З</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/z/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/I_(Cyrillic)" title="I (Cyrillic)">И</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Short_I" title="Short I">Й</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ka_(Cyrillic)" title="Ka (Cyrillic)">К</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/El_(Cyrillic)" title="El (Cyrillic)">Л</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Em_(Cyrillic)" title="Em (Cyrillic)">М</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/En_(Cyrillic)" title="En (Cyrillic)">Н</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/O_(Cyrillic)" title="O (Cyrillic)">О</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Pe_(Cyrillic)" title="Pe (Cyrillic)">П</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Er_(Cyrillic)" title="Er (Cyrillic)">Р</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/r/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Es_(Cyrillic)" title="Es (Cyrillic)">С</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Te_(Cyrillic)" title="Te (Cyrillic)">Т</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/U_(Cyrillic)" title="U (Cyrillic)">У</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ef_(Cyrillic)" title="Ef (Cyrillic)">Ф</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Kha_(Cyrillic)" title="Kha (Cyrillic)">Х</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Tse_(Cyrillic)" title="Tse (Cyrillic)">Ц</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ts/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Che_(Cyrillic)" title="Che (Cyrillic)">Ч</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tɕ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Sha_(Cyrillic)" title="Sha (Cyrillic)">Ш</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʂ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shcha_(Cyrillic)" title="Shcha (Cyrillic)">Щ</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɕː/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Hard_sign" title="Hard sign">Ъ</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Yery" title="Yery">Ы</a></span></span><br/> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɨ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Soft_sign" title="Soft sign">Ь</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʲ/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/E_(Cyrillic)" title="E (Cyrillic)">Э</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Yu_(Cyrillic)" title="Yu (Cyrillic)">Ю</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ju/</span></td>
<td><span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a href="/wiki/Ya_(Cyrillic)" title="Ya (Cyrillic)">Я</a></span></span><br/><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ja/</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Older letters of the Russian alphabet include ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѣ</span></span>⟩, which merged to ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">е</span></span>⟩ (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/je/</span> or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʲe/</span>); ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">і</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѵ</span></span>⟩, which both merged to ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">и</span></span>⟩ (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>); ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѳ</span></span>⟩, which merged to ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">ф</span></span>⟩ (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span>); ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѫ</span></span>⟩, which merged to ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">у</span></span>⟩ (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>); ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѭ</span></span>⟩, which merged to ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">ю</span></span>⟩ (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ju/</span> or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʲu/</span>); and ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѧ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Old East Slavic-language text"><span lang="orv">ѩ</span></span>⟩, which later were graphically reshaped into ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">я</span></span>⟩ and merged phonetically to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ja/</span> or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʲa/</span>. While these older letters have been abandoned at one time or another, they may be used in this and related articles. The <a href="/wiki/Yer" title="Yer">yers</a> ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">ъ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">ь</span></span>⟩ originally indicated the pronunciation of <i>ultra-short</i> or <i>reduced</i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŭ/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ĭ/</span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transliteration">Transliteration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Transliteration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">Romanization of Russian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Informal_romanizations_of_Russian" title="Informal romanizations of Russian">Informal romanizations of Russian</a></div>
<p>Because of many technical restrictions in computing and also because of the unavailability of Cyrillic keyboards abroad, Russian is often transliterated using the Latin alphabet. For example, <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B7#Russian" title="wikt:мороз">мороз</a></span> ('frost') is transliterated <i>moroz</i>, and  <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BC%D1%8B%D1%88%D1%8C#Russian" title="wikt:мышь">мышь</a></span> ('mouse'), <i>mysh</i> or <i>myš'</i>. Once commonly used by the majority of those living outside Russia, transliteration is being used less frequently by Russian-speaking typists in favor of the extension of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encoding</a>, which fully incorporates the Russian alphabet. Free programs are available offering this Unicode extension, which allow users to type Russian characters, even on Western 'QWERTY' keyboards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computing">Computing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Computing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Russian alphabet has many systems of <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encoding</a>. <a href="/wiki/KOI8-R" title="KOI8-R">KOI8-R</a> was designed by the Soviet government and was intended to serve as the standard encoding. This encoding was and still is widely used in UNIX-like operating systems. Nevertheless, the spread of <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> and <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> (<a href="/wiki/Code_page_866" title="Code page 866">IBM866</a>), traditional Macintosh (<a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_8859-5" title="ISO/IEC 8859-5">ISO/IEC 8859-5</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> (CP1251) meant the proliferation of many different encodings as de facto standards, with Windows-1251 becoming a <a href="/wiki/De_facto_standard" title="De facto standard">de facto standard</a> in Russian Internet and e-mail communication during the period of roughly 1995–2005.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>All the obsolete 8-bit encodings are rarely used in the communication protocols and text-exchange data formats, having been mostly replaced with <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">UTF-8</a>. A number of encoding conversion applications were developed. "<a href="/wiki/Iconv" title="Iconv">iconv</a>" is an example that is supported by most versions of <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">Macintosh</a> and some other <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>; but converters are rarely needed unless accessing texts created more than a few years ago.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In addition to the modern Russian alphabet, Unicode (and thus UTF-8) encodes the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Early Cyrillic alphabet">Early Cyrillic alphabet</a> (which is very similar to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a>), and all other Slavic and non-Slavic but Cyrillic-based alphabets.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russian_orthography" title="Russian orthography">Russian orthography</a></div>
<p>The current spelling follows the major reform of 1918, and the final codification of 1956. An update proposed in the late 1990s has met a hostile reception, and has not been formally adopted. The punctuation, originally based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a>, was in the 17th and 18th centuries reformulated on the French and German models.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>According to the Institute of Russian Language of the Russian Academy of Sciences, an optional <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">знак ударения</span></span>) may, and sometimes should, be used to mark <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>. For example, it is used to distinguish between otherwise identical words, especially when context does not make it obvious: <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">замо́к</span></span> (<i>zamók</i> – "lock") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">за́мок</span></span> (<i>zámok</i> – "castle"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">сто́ящий</span></span> (<i>stóyashchy</i> – "worthwhile") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">стоя́щий</span></span> (<i>stoyáshchy</i> – "standing"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">чудно́</span></span> (<i>chudnó</i> – "this is odd") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">чу́дно</span></span> (<i>chúdno</i> – "this is marvellous"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">молоде́ц</span></span> (<i>molodéts</i> – "well done!") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">мо́лодец</span></span> (<i>mólodets</i> – "fine young man"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">узна́ю</span></span> (<i>uznáyu</i> – "I shall learn it") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">узнаю́</span></span> (<i>uznayú</i> – "I recognize it"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">отреза́ть</span></span> (<i>otrezát</i> – "to be cutting") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">отре́зать</span></span> (<i>otrézat</i> – "to have cut"); to indicate the proper pronunciation of uncommon words, especially personal and family names, like <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">афе́ра</span></span> (<i>aféra</i>, "scandal, affair"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">гу́ру</span></span> (<i>gúru</i>, "guru"), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Гарси́я</span></span> (<i>García</i>), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Оле́ша</span></span> (<i>Olésha</i>), <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Фе́рми</span></span> (<i>Fermi</i>), and to show which is the stressed word in a sentence, for example <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Ты́ съел печенье?</span></span> (<i>Tý syel pechenye?</i> – "Was it <i>you</i> who ate the cookie?") – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Ты съе́л печенье?</span></span> (<i>Ty syél pechenye?</i> – "Did you <i>eat</i> the cookie?) – <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Ты съел пече́нье?</span></span> (<i>Ty syel pechénye?</i> "Was it the <i>cookie</i> you ate?"). Stress marks are mandatory in lexical dictionaries and books for children or Russian learners.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russian_phonology" title="Russian phonology">Russian phonology</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonological" title="Phonological">phonological</a> system of Russian is inherited from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_Slavonic" title="Common Slavonic">Common Slavonic</a>; it underwent considerable modification in the early historical period before being largely settled around the year 1400.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The language possesses five vowels (or six, under the St.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Petersburg Phonological School), which are written with different letters depending on whether the preceding consonant is <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalized</a>. The consonants typically come in plain vs. palatalized pairs, which are traditionally called <i>hard</i> and <i>soft.</i> The hard consonants are often <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarized</a>, especially before front vowels, as in <a href="/wiki/Irish_phonology#Consonants" title="Irish phonology">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marshallese_language#Phonology" title="Marshallese language">Marshallese</a>. The standard language, based on the Moscow dialect, possesses heavy stress and moderate variation in pitch. Stressed vowels are somewhat lengthened, while unstressed vowels tend to be reduced to near-close vowels or an unclear <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a>. (See also: <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction_in_Russian" title="Vowel reduction in Russian">vowel reduction in Russian</a>.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Russian <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a> structure can be quite complex, with both initial and final consonant clusters of up to four consecutive sounds. Using a formula with V standing for the nucleus (vowel) and C for each consonant, the maximal structure can be described as follows:
</p><p>(C)(C)(C)(C)V(C)(C)(C)(C)
</p><p>However, Russian has a constraint on syllabification such that syllables cannot span multiple <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Clusters of four consonants are not very common, especially within a morpheme. Some examples are: <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%B2%D0%B7%D0%B3%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B4#Russian" title="wikt:взгляд">взгляд</a></span> (<small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ru-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[vzglʲat]</a></span> <i>vzglyad</i>, 'glance'), <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%B3%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2#Russian" title="wikt:государств">государств</a></span> (<small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ru-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[gəsʊˈdarstf]</a></span> <i>gosudarstv</i>, 'of the states'), <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D1%82%D0%B2#Russian" title="wikt:строительств">строительств</a></span> (<small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ru-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[strɐˈitʲɪlʲstf]</a></span> <i>stroitelstv</i>, 'of the constructions').<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<p>Russian is notable for its distinction based on <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalization</a> of most of its consonants. While <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ts, k, ɡ, x/</span> do have true palatalized <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tsʲ, kʲ, ɡʲ, xʲ]</span>, only <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kʲ/</span> might be considered a phoneme, though it is marginal and generally not considered distinctive. The only native <a href="/wiki/Minimal_pair" title="Minimal pair">minimal pair</a> that argues for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kʲ/</span> being a separate phoneme is <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">это <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D1%82%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%82%D1%8C#Russian" title="wikt:ткать">ткёт</a></span></span></span> (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɛtə tkʲɵt]</span> <i>eto tkyot</i> – "it weaves") –  <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">этот <span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82#Russian" title="wikt:кот">кот</a></span></span></span> (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɛtət kot]</span>, <i>etot kot</i> – "this cat"). The phoneme /<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ts</span>/ is generally considered to be always hard; however, loan words such as <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Цюрих</a> and some other neologisms contain /<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">tsʲ</span>/ through the word-building processes (e.g. фрицёнок, шпицята). Palatalization means that the center of the tongue is raised during and after the articulation of the consonant. In the case of <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʲ/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dʲ/</span>, the tongue is raised enough to produce slight frication (affricate sounds; cf. Belarusian ць, дзь, or Polish ć, dź). The sounds <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t, d, ts, s, z, n, rʲ/</span> are <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental</a>, that is, pronounced with the tip of the tongue against the teeth rather than against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_ridge" title="Alveolar ridge">alveolar ridge</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; float: left; margin-right: 1em;">

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_vowel_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="980" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Russian_vowel_chart.svg/220px-Russian_vowel_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Russian_vowel_chart.svg/330px-Russian_vowel_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Russian_vowel_chart.svg/440px-Russian_vowel_chart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russian_vowel_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian vowel chart by <a href="#CITEREFTrofimovJones1923">Trofimov &amp; Jones (1923</a>:55)</div></div></div>
<p>Russian has five or six vowels in stressed syllables, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i, u, e, o, a/</span>, and in some analyses <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɨ/</span>, but in most cases these vowels have merged to only two to four vowels when unstressed: <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i, u, a/</span> (or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɨ, u, a/</span>) after hard consonants and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i, u/</span> after soft ones. These vowels have several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allophones" title="Allophones">allophones</a>, which are displayed on the diagram to the right.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Russian_grammar" title="Russian grammar">Russian grammar</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2014</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Russian has preserved an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a>-<a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> structure, although considerable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphological_levelling" title="Morphological levelling">levelling</a> has occurred.
Russian grammar encompasses:
</p>
<ul><li>a highly <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional</a> <b>morphology</b></li>
<li>a <b>syntax</b> that, for the literary language, is the conscious fusion of three elements:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[95]</a></sup>
<ul><li>a polished <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> foundation;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Church Slavonic</a> inheritance;</li>
<li>a Western European style.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>The spoken language has been influenced by the literary one but continues to preserve characteristic forms. The dialects show various non-standard grammatical features,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[96]</a></sup> some of which are archaisms or descendants of old forms since discarded by the literary language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In terms of actual grammar, there are three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenses" title="Tenses">tenses</a> in Russian –  past, present, and future –  and each verb has two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect_in_Slavic_languages" title="Grammatical aspect in Slavic languages">aspects</a> (perfective and imperfective). Russian nouns each have a gender –  either feminine, masculine, or neuter, chiefly indicated by spelling at the end of the word. Words change depending on both their gender and function in the sentence. Russian has six <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">cases</a>: Nominative (for the grammatical subject), Accusative (for direct objects), Dative (for indirect objects), Genitive (to indicate possession or relation), Instrumental (to indicate 'with' or 'by means of'), and Prepositional (used after the locative prepositions в "in", на "on", о "about", при "in the presence of"). Verbs of motion in Russian –  such as 'go', 'walk', 'run', 'swim', and 'fly' –  use the imperfective or perfective form to indicate a single or return trip, and also use a multitude of <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefixes</a> to add shades of meaning to the verb. Such verbs also take on different forms to distinguish between concrete and abstract motion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="1013" decoding="async" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg/220px-Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg/330px-Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg/440px-Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karion_Istomin%27s_alphabet_P.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This page from an "ABC" book printed in Moscow in 1694 shows the letter <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D0%9F" title="П">П</a></b>.</div></div></div>
<p>See <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russian_language" title="History of the Russian language">History of the Russian language</a> for an account of the successive foreign influences on Russian.

</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_examples">History and examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History and examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russian_language" title="History of the Russian language">History of the Russian language</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reforms_of_Russian_orthography" title="Reforms of Russian orthography">Reforms of Russian orthography</a></div>
<p>The history of the Russian language may be divided into the following periods:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russian_language#Kievan_period_and_feudal_breakup" title="History of the Russian language">Kievan period and feudal breakup</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Russian" title="Early Modern Russian">The Moscow period (15th–17th centuries)</a></li>
<li>Standard national language</li></ul>
<p>Judging by the historical records, by approximately 1000 AD the predominant ethnic group over much of modern European Russia, Ukraine, and Belarus was the Eastern branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavs</a>, speaking a closely related group of dialects. The political unification of this region into <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a> in about 880, from which modern Russia, Ukraine, and Belarus trace their origins, established <a href="/wiki/Old_East_Slavic" title="Old East Slavic">Old East Slavic</a> as a literary and commercial language. It was soon followed by the adoption of Christianity in 988 and the introduction of the South Slavic <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> as the liturgical and official language. Borrowings and <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calques</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a> began to enter the Old East Slavic and spoken dialects at this time, which in their turn modified the Old Church Slavonic as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ostromirovo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="596" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ostromirovo.jpg/220px-Ostromirovo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ostromirovo.jpg/330px-Ostromirovo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Ostromirovo.jpg/440px-Ostromirovo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ostromirovo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ostromir_Gospels" title="Ostromir Gospels">Ostromir Gospels</a> of 1056 is the second oldest <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">East Slavic</a> book known, one of many medieval <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> preserved in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_National_Library" title="Russian National Library">Russian National Library</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Dialectal differentiation accelerated after the breakup of Kievan Rus' in approximately 1100. On the territories of modern Belarus and Ukraine emerged <a href="/wiki/Ruthenian_language" title="Ruthenian language">Ruthenian</a> and in modern Russia <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Russian_language" title="History of the Russian language">medieval Russian</a>. They became distinct since the 13th century, i.e. following the division of the land between the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a> in the west and independent <a href="/wiki/Novgorod_Republic" title="Novgorod Republic">Novgorod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pskov_Republic" title="Pskov Republic">Pskov</a> feudal republics plus numerous small duchies (which came to be vassals of the <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a>) in the east.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The official language in Moscow and Novgorod, and later, in the growing Muscovy, was <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic" title="Church Slavonic">Church Slavonic</a>, which evolved from Old Church Slavonic and remained <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">the literary language</a> for centuries, until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia#Early_reign" title="Peter I of Russia">Petrine age</a>, when its usage became limited to biblical and liturgical texts. Russian developed under a strong influence of Church Slavonic until the close of the 17th century; afterward the influence reversed, leading to corruption of liturgical texts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The political reforms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" title="Peter I of Russia">Peter the Great</a> (Пётр Вели́кий, <i>Pyótr Velíky</i>) were accompanied by a reform of the alphabet, and achieved their goal of secularization and Westernization. Blocks of specialized vocabulary were adopted from the languages of Western Europe. By 1800, a significant portion of the gentry spoke French daily, and German sometimes. Many Russian novels of the 19th century, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>'s (Лев Толсто́й) <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i>, contain entire paragraphs and even pages in French with no translation given, with an assumption that educated readers would not need one.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The modern literary language is usually considered to date from the time of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pushkin" title="Alexander Pushkin">Alexander Pushkin</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Алекса́ндр Пу́шкин</span></span>) in the first third of the 19th century. Pushkin revolutionized <a href="/wiki/Russian_literature" title="Russian literature">Russian literature</a> by rejecting archaic grammar and vocabulary (so-called <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">высо́кий стиль</span></span> — "high style") in favor of grammar and vocabulary found in the spoken language of the time. Even modern readers of younger age may only experience slight difficulties understanding some words in Pushkin's texts, since relatively few words used by Pushkin have become archaic or changed meaning. In fact, many expressions used by Russian writers of the early 19th century, in particular Pushkin, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lermontov" title="Mikhail Lermontov">Mikhail Lermontov</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Михаи́л Ле́рмонтов</span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Gogol" title="Nikolai Gogol">Nikolai Gogol</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Никола́й Го́голь</span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksander_Griboyedov" title="Aleksander Griboyedov">Aleksander Griboyedov</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Алекса́ндр Грибое́дов</span></span>), became proverbs or sayings which can be frequently found even in modern Russian colloquial speech.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint">

<p>The political upheavals of the early 20th century and the wholesale changes of political ideology gave written Russian its modern appearance after the <a href="/wiki/Reforms_of_Russian_orthography" title="Reforms of Russian orthography">spelling reform of 1918</a>. Political circumstances and Soviet accomplishments in military, scientific, and technological matters (especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonautics</a>), gave Russian a worldwide prestige, especially during the mid-20th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During the Soviet period, the policy toward the languages of the various other ethnic groups fluctuated in practice. Though each of the constituent republics had its own official language, the unifying role and superior status was reserved for Russian, although it was declared the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> only in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[100]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">break-up of the USSR</a> in 1991, several of the newly independent states have encouraged their native languages, which has partly reversed the privileged status of Russian, though its role as the language of post-Soviet national discourse throughout the region has continued.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Russian language in the world declined after 1991 due to the collapse of the Soviet Union and decrease in the number of <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> in the world and diminution of the total population in Russia (where Russian is an official language), however this has since been reversed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope.ru_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope.ru-58">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20130215-1_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20130215-1-111">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mof.gov.cy_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mof.gov.cy-112">[102]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>According to figures published in 2006 in the journal "<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF_Weekly" title="ru:Демоскоп Weekly">Demoskop Weekly</a>" research deputy director of Research Center for Sociological Research of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_and_Science_(Russia)" title="Ministry of Education and Science (Russia)">Ministry of Education and Science (Russia)</a> Arefyev A. L.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[103]</a></sup> the Russian language is gradually losing its position in the world in general, and in Russia in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20130215-1_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20130215-1-111">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-115">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[106]</a></sup> In 2012, A. L. Arefyev published a new study "Russian language at the turn of the 20th–21st centuries", in which he confirmed his conclusion about the trend of weakening of the Russian language after the Soviet Union's collapse in various regions of the world (findings published in 2013 in the journal "<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BF_Weekly" title="ru:Демоскоп Weekly">Demoskop Weekly</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope.ru_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope.ru-58">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20130213-1_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20130213-1-119">[109]</a></sup> In the countries of the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> the Russian language was being replaced or used in conjunction with local languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope.ru_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope.ru-58">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[110]</a></sup> Currently, the number of speakers of Russian in the world depends on the number of <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> in the world and total population in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demoscope.ru_58-4"><a href="#cite_note-demoscope.ru-58">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20130215-1_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20130215-1-111">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mof.gov.cy_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mof.gov.cy-112">[102]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-UAConstitution-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-UAConstitution_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.rada.gov.ua/const/conengl.htm#r1" rel="nofollow">Article 10</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110521190059/http://www.rada.gov.ua/const/conengl.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> May 21, 2011, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> of the Constitution says: "The state language of Ukraine is the Ukrainian language. The State ensures the comprehensive development and functioning of the Ukrainian language in all spheres of social life throughout the entire territory of Ukraine. In Ukraine, the free development, use and protection of Russian, and other languages of national minorities of Ukraine, is guaranteed."</span>
